Term Limits, Inc.</em> v. <em>Thornton</em>, 514 U.&nbsp;S. 779, 822, n. 32 (1995). <em> </em>One year later, it held unconstitutional a ballot initiative that would have prevented the enactment of laws under which "&nbsp;'homosexual, lesbian or bisexual orientation, conduct, practices or relationships [would] constitute or otherwise be the basis of .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. any minority status, quota preferences, protected status or claim of discrimination.'&nbsp;"  <em>Romer</em> v. <em>Evans</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 620, 624 (1996).  The Court neither gave deference to state lawmaking nor said anything about the virtues of direct democracy.  It instead declared that the result of the ballot initiative was an aberration--that "[i]t is not within our constitutional tradition to enact laws of this sort."  <em>Id.,</em> at 633.  But if "constitutional tradition" is the measuring stick, then it is hard to understand how the Court condones a redistricting practice that was unheard of for nearly 200 years after the ratification of the Constitution and that conflicts with the express constitutional command that election laws "be prescribed in each State by the Legislature thereof," Art.&nbsp;I, 4.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court's lack of respect for ballot initiatives is evident not only in what it has done, but in what it has failed to do.  Just this Term, the Court repeatedly refused to review cases in which the Courts of Appeals had set aside state laws passed through ballot initiative.  See, <em>e.g., County of Maricopa </em>v. <em>Lopez-Valenzuela</em>, 575 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2015) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., dissenting from denial of certiorari) (state constitutional amendment denying bail for illegal aliens arrested in certain circumstances); <em>Herbert </em>v. <em>Kitchen</em>, 574 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2014) (state constitutional amendment retaining traditional definition of marriage); <em>Smith </em>v. <em>Bishop</em>, 574 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2014) (same); <em>Rainey </em>v. <em>Bostic</em>, 574 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2014) (same); <em>Walker </em>v. <em>Wolf</em>, 574 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2014) (same).  It did so despite warnings that its indifference to such cases would "only embolden the lower courts to reject state laws on questionable constitutional grounds."  <em>Lopez-Valenzuela, supra, </em>at ___ (slip op., at 2).  And it refused to grant a stay pending appeal of a decision purporting to require the State of Alabama to issue marriage licenses to same-sex couples, even though Alabama's licensing laws had not been challenged in that case.  See <em>Strange </em>v. <em>Searcy</em>, 574 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2015) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas, J., </small></span>dissenting from denial of application for stay).  In each decision, the cheers for direct democracy were conspicuously absent.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Sometimes disapproval of ballot initiatives has been even more blatant.  Just last Term, one dissenting opinion castigated the product of a state ballot initiative as "stymieing the right of racial minorities to participate in the political process."  <em>Schuette </em>v. <em>BAMN</em>, 572 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2014) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J., joined by <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span>, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 1).  It did not hail the ballot initiative as the result of a "State's empowerment of its people," <em>ante,</em> at 19, nor offer any deference to state lawmaking.  Instead, it complained that "[t]he majority of Michigan voters changed the rules in the middle of the game, reconfiguring the existing political process .&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;."  <em>Schuette</em>, 572 U.&nbsp;S.,  at ___ (slip op., at 4).  And it criticized state ballot initiatives as biased against racial minorities because such minorities "face an especially uphill battle" in seeking the passage of such initiatives.  <em>Id.</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 20).  How quickly the tune has changed.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And how striking that it changed here.  The ballot initiative in this case, unlike those that the Court has previously treated so dismissively, was unusually democracy-reducing.  It did not ask the people to approve a particular redistricting plan through direct democracy, but instead to take districting away from the people's representatives and give it to an unelected committee, thereby reducing democratic control over the process in the future.  The Court's characterization of this as direct democracy at its best is rather like praising a plebiscite in a "banana republic" that installs a strongman as President for Life.  And wrapping the analysis in a cloak of federalism does little to conceal the flaws in the Court's reasoning.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I would dispense with the faux federalism and would instead treat the States in an evenhanded manner.  That means applying the Constitution as written.  Although the straightforward text of Article I, 4, prohibits redistricting by an unelected, independent commission, Article III limits our power to deciding cases or controversies.  Because I agree with <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia </small></span>that the Arizona Legislature lacks Article III standing to assert an institutional injury against another entity of state government, I would dismiss its suit. I respectfully dissent.</p>
<p></p><hr>


<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The term "gerrymander" is a portmanteau of the last name of Elbridge Gerry, the eighth Governor of Massachusetts, and the shape of the electoral map he famously contorted for partisan gain, which included one district shaped like a salamander.  See E. Griffith, The Rise and Development of the Gerrymander 16-19 (Arno ed. 1974).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Massachusetts Constitution of 1780 is illustrative of the understanding that the people's authority could trump the state legislature's.  Framed by a separate convention, it was submitted to the people for ratification.  That occurred after the legislature attempted to promulgate a Constitution it had written, an endeavor that drew opposition from many Massachusetts towns.  See J. Rakove, Original Meanings: Politics and Ideas in the Making of the Constitution 96-101 (1996); G. Wood, The Creation of the American Republic, 1776-1787, pp. 339-341 (1969).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The people's sovereign right to incorporate themselves into a State's lawmaking apparatus, by reserving for themselves the power to adopt laws and to veto measures passed by elected representatives, is one this Court has ranked a nonjusticiable political matter.  <em>Pacific States Telephone &amp; Telegraph Co.</em> v. <em>Oregon</em>, 223 U.&nbsp;S. 118 (1912) (rejecting challenge to referendum mounted under Article IV, 4's undertaking by the United States to "guarantee to every State in th[e] Union a Republican Form of Government").  But see <em>New York</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S. 144, 185 (1992) ("[P]erhaps not all claims under the Guarantee Clause present nonjusticiable political questions.").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;From Arizona's admission to the Union in 1912 to 1940, no congressional districting occurred because Arizona had only one Member of Congress.  K. Martis, The Historical Atlas of United States Congressional Districts, 1789-1983, p.&nbsp;3 (1982) (Table 1).  Court-ordered congressional districting plans were in place from 1966 to 1970, and from 1982 through 2000.  See <em>Klahr</em> v. <em>Williams</em>, 313 F.&nbsp;Supp. 148 (Ariz. 1970); <em>Goddard</em> v. <em>Babbitt</em>, 536 F.&nbsp;Supp. 538 (Ariz. 1982); <em>Arizonans for Fair Representation</em> v. <em>Symington</em>, 828 F.&nbsp;Supp. 684 (Ariz. 1992); Norrander &amp; Wendland, Redistricting in Arizona, in Reapportionment and Redistricting in the West 177, 178-179 (G. Moncrief ed. 2011).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In the current climate of heightened partisanship, the AIRC has encountered interference with its operations.  In particular, its dependence on the Arizona Legislature for funding, and the removal provision have proved problematic.  In 2011, when the AIRC proposed boundaries the majority party did not like, the Governor of Arizona attempted to remove the Commission's independent chair.  Her attempt was stopped by the Arizona Supreme Court.  See Cain, Redistricting Commissions:  A Better Political Buffer?  121 Yale L.&nbsp;J. 1808, 1835-1836 (2012) (citing <em>Mathis</em> v. <em>Brewer</em>, No. CV-11-0313-SA (Ariz. 2011)); <em>Arizona Independent Redistricting Comm'n</em> v. <em>Brewer</em>, 229 Ariz. 347, 275 P.&nbsp;3d 1267 (2012).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See<em> </em>Haw. Const., Art. IV, 2, and Haw. Rev. Stat. 25-1 to 25-9 (2009 and 2013 Cum. Supp.); Idaho Const., Art. III, 2; Mont. Const., Art. V, 14; N.&nbsp;J. Const., Art. II, 2; Wash Const., Art. II, 43.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Cal. Const., Art. XXI, 2; Cal. Govt. Code Ann. 8251-8253.6 (West Supp. 2015).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Iowa Code 42.1-42.6 (2013); Ohio Rev. Code Ann. 103.51 (Lexis 2014); Me. Const., Art. IV, pt. 3, 1-A.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Conn. Const., Art. III, 6; Ind. Code 3-3-2-2 (2014).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p><em>Massachusetts</em> v. <em>Mellon</em>, 262 U.&nbsp;S. 447 (1923), featured in <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span>'s dissent, <em>post</em>, at 4, bears little resemblance to this case.  There, the Court unanimously found that Massachusetts lacked standing to sue the Secretary of the Treasury on a claim that a federal grant program exceeded Congress' Article I powers and thus violated the Tenth Amendment.  <em>Id.</em>, at 480.  If suing on its own behalf, the Court reasoned, Massachusetts' claim involved no "quasi-sovereign rights actually invaded or threatened."  <em>Id.</em>, at 485.  As <em>parens patriae</em>, the Court stated: "[I]t is no part of [Massachusetts'] duty or power to enforce [its citizens'] rights in respect of their relations with the Federal Government.  In that field it is the United States, and not the State, which represents them as <em>parens patriae</em>."  <em>Id.</em>, at 485-486.  As astutely observed, moreover: "The cases on the standing of states to sue the federal government seem to depend on the kind of claim that the state advances.  The decisions .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. are hard to reconcile."  R. Fallon, J. Manning, D. Meltzer, &amp; D. Shapiro, Hart and Wechsler's The Federal Courts and the Federal System 263-266 (6th ed. 2009) (comparing <em>Mellon</em> with <em>South Carolina</em> v. <em>Katzenbach</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S. 301, 308 (1966) (rejecting on the merits the claim that the Voting Rights Act of 1965 invaded reserved powers of the States to determine voter qualifications and regulate elections), <em>Nebraska</em> v. <em>Wyoming</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 20 (1995) (recognizing that Wyoming could bring suit to vindicate the State's "quasi-sovereign" interests in the physical environment within its domain (emphasis deleted; internal quotation marks omitted)), and <em>Massachusetts</em> v. <em>EPA</em>, 549 U.&nbsp;S. 497, 520 (2007) (maintaining that Massachusetts "is entitled to special solicitude in our standing analysis")).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Coleman</em> concerned the proposed Child Labor Amendment, which provided that "Congress shall have power to limit, regulate, and prohibit the labor of persons under eighteen years of age."  307 U.&nbsp;S., at 435, n.&nbsp;1 (internal quotation marks omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The case before us does not touch or concern the question whether Congress has standing to bring a suit against the President.  There is no federal analogue to Arizona's initiative power, and a suit between Congress and the President would raise separation-of-powers concerns absent here.  The Court's standing analysis, we have noted, has been "especially rigorous when reaching the merits of the dispute would force [the Court] to decide whether an action taken by one of the other two branches of the Federal Government was unconstitutional."  <em>Raines</em> v. <em>Byrd</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 811, 819-820 (1997).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In an endeavor to wish away <em>Coleman</em>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span>, in dissent, suggests the case may have been "a 4-to-4 standoff."  <em>Post</em>, at 5.  He overlooks that Chief Justice Hughes' opinion, announced by Justice Stone, was styled "Opinion of the Court."  307 U.&nbsp;S., at 435.  Describing <em>Coleman</em>, the Court wrote in <em>Raines</em>:  "By a vote of 5-4, we held that [the 20 Kansas Senators who voted against ratification of a proposed federal constitutional amendment] had standing."  521 U.&nbsp;S., at  822.  For opinions recognizing the precedential weight of <em>Coleman</em>, see <em>Baker</em> v. <em>Carr</em>, 369 U.&nbsp;S. 186, 208 (1962); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Windsor</em>, 570 U. S. ___, ___ (2013) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito, J.</small></span>, dissenting) (slip op., at 4-5).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Curiously, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span>, dissenting on standing, berates the Court for "treading upon the powers of state legislatures."  <em>Post</em>, at 6.  He forgets that the party invoking federal-court jurisdiction in this case, and inviting our review, is the Arizona State Legislature.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span>, on the way to deciding that the Arizona Legislature lacks standing, first addresses the merits.  In so doing, he overlooks that, in the cases he features, it was entirely immaterial whether the law involved was adopted by a representative body or by the people, through exercise of the initiative.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNopinion1.16" href="#FRopinion1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Seventeenth Amendment provided for election of Senators "by the people" of each State.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNopinion1.17" href="#FRopinion1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The list of constitutional provisions in which the word "legislature" appears, appended to <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span>'s opinion, <em>post</em>, at 28-32, is illustrative of the variety of functions state legislatures can be called upon to exercise.  For example, Art.&nbsp;I, 2, cl.&nbsp;1, superseded by the Seventeenth Amendment, assigned an "electoral" function.  See <em>Smiley</em>, 285 U.&nbsp;S., at 365.  Article I, 3, cl.&nbsp;2, assigns an "appointive" function.  Article I, 8, cl. 17, assigns a "consenting" function, see <em>Smiley</em>, 285 U.&nbsp;S., at 366, as does Art.&nbsp;IV, 3, cl.&nbsp;1.  "[R]atifying" functions are assigned in Art.&nbsp;V, Amdt. 18, 3, Amdt. 20, 6, and Amdt. 22, 2.  See <em>Hawke</em>, 253 U.&nbsp;S., at 229.  But Art.&nbsp;I, 4, cl.&nbsp;1, unquestionably calls for the exercise of lawmaking authority.  That authority can be carried out by a representative body, but if a State so chooses, legislative authority can also be lodged in the people themselves.  See <em>infra</em>, at 24-35.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNopinion1.18" href="#FRopinion1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The AIRC referenced 2a(c) in briefing below, see Motion to Dismiss&nbsp;8-9, and Response to Plaintiff's Motion for Preliminary Injunction 12-14, in No. 12-1211 (D Ariz.), and in its motion to dismiss or affirm in this Court, see Motion to Dismiss or Affirm 28-31.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNopinion1.19" href="#FRopinion1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The 1911 Act also required States to comply with certain federally prescribed districting rules--namely, that Representatives be elected "by districts composed of a contiguous and compact territory, and containing as nearly as practicable an equal number of inhabitants," and that the districts "be equal to the number of Representatives to which [the] State may be entitled in Congress, no district electing more than one Representative."  Act of Aug. 8, 1911, ch. 5, 3-4, 37 Stat. 14.  When a State's apportionment of Representatives remained constant, the Act directed the State to continue using its pre-existing districts "until [the] State shall be redistricted as herein prescribed."  See 4, <em>ibid.</em>  The 1911 Act did not address redistricting in the event a State's apportionment of Representatives decreased, likely because no State faced a decrease following the 1910 census.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNopinion1.20" href="#FRopinion1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Because a State is required to comply with the Federal Constitution, the Voting Rights Act, and other federal laws when it draws and implements its district map, nothing in 2a(c) affects a challenge to a state district map on the ground that it violates one or more of those federal requirements.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNopinion1.21" href="#FRopinion1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The plurality in <em>Branch</em> v. <em>Smith</em>, 538 U.&nbsp;S. 254, 273 (2003), considered the question whether 2a(c) had been repealed by implication and stated, "where what it prescribes is constitutional," the provision "continues to apply."</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span>, in dissent, insists that 2a(c) and its precursor, the 1911 Act, have nothing to do with this case.  <em>Post</em>, at 20-21, 23.  Undeniably, however, it was the very purpose of the measures to recognize the legislative authority each State has to determine its own redistricting regime.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNopinion1.23" href="#FRopinion1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Illustrative of an embracive comprehension of the word "legislature," Charles Pinckney explained at South Carolina's ratifying convention that America is "[a] republic, where the people at large, either collectively or by representation, form the legislature."  4 Debates on the Federal Constitution 328 (J. Elliot 2d ed. 1863).  Participants in the debates over the Elections Clause used the word "legislature" interchangeably with "state" and "state government."  See Brief for Brennan Center for Justice at N. Y. U. School of Law as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 6-7.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNopinion1.24" href="#FRopinion1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span>, in dissent, cites <em>U.&nbsp;S. Term Limits, Inc.</em> v. <em>Thornton</em>, 514 U.&nbsp;S. 779 (1995), as an important precedent we overlook.  <em>Post</em>, at 24-25.  There, we held that state-imposed term limits on candidates for the House and Senate violated the Clauses of the Constitution setting forth qualifications for membership in Congress, Art.&nbsp;I, 2, cl.&nbsp;2, and Art.&nbsp;I, 3, cl.&nbsp;3.  We did so for a reason entirely harmonious with today's decision.  Adding state-imposed limits to the qualifications set forth in the Constitution, the Court wrote, would be "contrary to the 'fundamental principle of our representative democracy,' .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. that 'the people should choose whom they please to govern them.'&nbsp;"  514 U.&nbsp;S., at 783 (quoting <em>Powell</em> v. <em>McCormack</em>, 395 U.&nbsp;S. 486, 547 (1969)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A State may choose to regulate state and national elections differently, which is its prerogative under the Clause.  <em>E.g.</em>, Ind. Code 3-3-2-2 (creating backup commission for congressional but not state legislative districts).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNopinion1.26" href="#FRopinion1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See App. to Brief for Appellees 11a-29a (collecting state constitutional provisions governing elections).  States' constitutional conventions are not simply past history predating the first election of state legislatures.  Louisiana, for example, held the most recent of its 12 constitutional conventions in 1992.  J. Dinan, The American State Constitutional Tradition 8-9 (2006) (Table 1-1).  The State's provision for voting by "secret ballot" may be traced to the constitutional convention held by the State in 1812, see La. Const., Art. VI, 13, but was most recently reenacted at the State's 1974 constitutional convention, see Art. XI, 2.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNopinion1.27" href="#FRopinion1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Madison called the decision "[w]hether the electors should vote by ballot or viv voce" a quintessential subject of regulation under the Elections Clause.  2 Records of the Federal Convention 240-241 (M. Farrand rev. 1966).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNopinion1.28" href="#FRopinion1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Miss. Const., Art. XII, 249; N.&nbsp;C. Const., Art. VI, 3; Va. Const., Art. II, 2; W.&nbsp;Va. Const., Art. IV, 12; Wash. Const., Art. VI, 7.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 29" name="FNopinion1.29" href="#FRopinion1.29">29</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>E.g.</em>, Haw. Const., Art. II, 4; La. Const., Art XI, 2; N.&nbsp;D. Const., Art. II, 1; Pa. Const., Art. VII, 14.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 30" name="FNopinion1.30" href="#FRopinion1.30">30</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>E.g.</em>, Ark. Const., Art. III, 11 (ballots unlawfully not counted in the first instance must be counted after election); La. Const., Art XI, 2 (all ballots must be counted publicly).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 31" name="FNopinion1.31" href="#FRopinion1.31">31</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>E.g.</em>, Ariz. Const., Art. VII, 7 (setting plurality of votes as the standard for victory in all elections, excluding runoffs); Mont. Const., Art. IV, 5 (same); Ore. Const., Art. II, 16 (same).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This provision was modified by the Seventeenth Amendment.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The only hint of support the majority can glean from precedent is a passing reference in <em>Atlantic Cleaners &amp; Dyers, Inc.</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 286 U.&nbsp;S. 427, 434 (1932), a case about how to interpret "trade or commerce" in the Sherman Act.  See <em>ante</em>, at 18.  And even that selected snippet describes the "legislature" as a "body."  286 U.&nbsp;S., at 434.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority's suggestion that <em>Baldwin</em> should be dismissed as an act of partisanship appears to have no basis, unless one is willing to regard as tainted every decision in favor of a candidate from the same party as a majority of the Elections Committee.  <em>Ante</em>, at 29-30.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Not surprisingly, Section 2a(c) was barely raised below and was not addressed by the District Court.  See <em>ante</em>, at 19, n.&nbsp;18.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority is prepared to say that Section 2a(c) has more than "nothing to do with this case."  <em>Ante</em>, at 23, n. 22.  Not exactly a ringing endorsement.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Term Limits</em> was of course not decided on the abstract principle that "the people should choose whom they please to govern them."  <em>Ante</em>, at 27, n. 24 (quoting 514 U.&nbsp;S., at 783).  If that were the rule, the people could choose a 20-year-old Congressman, a 25-year-old Senator, or a foreign President.  But see Art.&nbsp;I, 2, cl.&nbsp;2; 3, cl.&nbsp;3; Art.&nbsp;II, 1, cl.&nbsp;5.  <em>Term Limits</em> instead relied on analysis of the text, structure, and history of the Constitution--all factors that cut strongly against the majority's position today.</p>

</main><script>_satellite["_runScript1"](function(event, target, Promise) {
_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}
